million (2012)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e21_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-e21-3">[3]</a></sup><br/>Combined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_language" title="Native language">L1</a> and <li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg/20px-Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg/31px-Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg/40px-Flag_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">DR Congo</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Mozambique.svg/23px-Flag_of_Mozambique.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Mozambique.svg/35px-Flag_of_Mozambique.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Mozambique.svg/45px-Flag_of_Mozambique.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a></li></ul>
</div></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_language_regulators" title="List of language regulators">Regulated by</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span lang="sw" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Baraza_la_Kiswahili_la_Taifa" title="Baraza la Kiswahili la Taifa">Baraza la Kiswahili la Taifa</a></span></span> (Tanzania)</li>
<li><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span lang="sw" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Chama_cha_Kiswahili_cha_Taifa" title="Chama cha Kiswahili cha Taifa">Chama cha Kiswahili cha Taifa</a></span></span> (Kenya)</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="color: black; background-color:  #ffddaa;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" </div></code><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guthrie_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guthrie-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code>99-AUS-m</code></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png"><img alt="Maeneo penye wasemaji wa Kiswahili.png" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="383" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png/220px-Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png/330px-Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Maeneo_penye_wasemaji_wa_Kiswahili.png 2x" width="220"/></a><div style="text-align:left;">Geographic-administrative extent of Swahili. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="background:#fff6d9"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="background:#fee8ab">Person</th><td class="infobox-data">Mswahili</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="background:#fee8ab">People</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Waswahili</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="background:#fee8ab">Language</th><td class="infobox-data">Kiswahili</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Swahili</b>, also known by its <a href="/wiki/Endonym_and_exonym" title="Endonym and exonym">native name</a> <b><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">Kiswahili</i></span></b>, is the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a>, who are found primarily in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (along the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African</a> coast and adjacent litoral islands).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Although linguistically categorised as a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu language</a>, Swahili developed historically by borrowing a number of words from foreign languages, particularly <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, but also words from <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>. Around forty percent of the Swahili vocabulary consists of Arabic loanwords,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-9">[9]</a></sup> including the name of the language (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">سَوَاحِلي</span></span> <span title="Arabic-language text"><i lang="ar-Latn">sawāḥilī</i></span>, a plural adjectival form of an Arabic word meaning 'of the coast'). The loanwords date from the era of contact between <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave traders</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Bantu_languages" title="Northeast Bantu languages">Bantu</a> inhabitants of the east coast of Africa, which was also the time period when Swahili emerged as a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The number of Swahili speakers, be they native or second-language speakers, is estimated to be approximately 200 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-standford_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-standford-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to concerted efforts by the government of <a href="/wiki/Baraza_la_Kiswahili_la_Taifa" title="Baraza la Kiswahili la Taifa">Tanzania</a>, Swahili is one of three official languages (the others being English and French) of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> (EAC) countries, namely <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of other areas in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, including some parts of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> (DRC), <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, the southern tip of <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Swahili is also one of the working <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_African_Union" title="Languages of the African Union">languages of the African Union</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a>. The <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> created an institution called the <i>East African Kiswahili Commission</i> (EACK) which began operations in 2015. The institution currently serves as the leading body for promoting the language in the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African region</a>, as well as for coordinating its development and usage for regional integration and sustainable development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Press_Release_on_EAKC_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Release_on_EAKC-16">[16]</a></sup> In recent years <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> have begun offering Swahili as a subject in schools or have developed plans to do so.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comorian_language" title="Comorian language">Shikomor</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comorian_language" title="Comorian language">Comorian</a>), an official language in <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a> and also spoken in <a href="/wiki/Mayotte" title="Mayotte">Mayotte</a> (<span title="Maore Comorian-language text"><i lang="swb"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shimaore" title="Shimaore">Shimaore</a></i></span>), is closely related to Swahili and is sometimes considered a dialect of Swahili, although other authorities consider it a distinct language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In 2022, based on Swahili's growth as a prominent international language, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> declared <a href="/wiki/UN_Swahili_Language_Day" title="UN Swahili Language Day">Swahili Language Day</a> as 7 July to commenorate the date that <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> adopted the Swahili as a unifying language for African independence struggles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Swahili is a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki branch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Guthrie_classification_of_Bantu_languages" title="Guthrie classification of Bantu languages">Guthrie's geographic classification</a>, Swahili is in Bantu zone G, whereas the other Sabaki languages are in zone E70, commonly under the name <i>Nyika.</i> Historical linguists consider the Arabic influence on Swahili to be significant, since it takes around 40% of its vocabulary directly from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and was initially spread by <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave traders</a> along <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>'s coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1723" data-file-width="1006" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/170px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/255px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/340px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swahili in Arabic script—memorial plate at the <a href="/wiki/Askari_Monument" title="Askari Monument">Askari Monument</a>, Dar es Salaam (1927)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origin of the word Swahili is its phonetic equivalent in Arabic:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The core of the Swahili language originates in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Coast_Bantu_languages" title="Northeast Coast Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> of the coast of East Africa. Much of Swahili's Bantu vocabulary has cognates in the <a href="/wiki/Pokomo_language" title="Pokomo language">Pokomo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taita_language" title="Taita language">Taita</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mijikenda_language" title="Mijikenda language">Mijikenda languages</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and, to a lesser extent, other <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Bantu_languages" title="Northeast Bantu languages">East African Bantu languages</a>. While opinions vary on the specifics, it has been historically purported that around half of the Swahili vocabulary is derived from loan words, the vast majority <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, but also other contributing languages, including <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Source languages for loanwords in Swahili<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Source languages</th>
<th>Percentage
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Arabic (mainly <a href="/wiki/Omani_Arabic" title="Omani Arabic">Omani Arabic</a>)</td>
<td>40%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>English</td>
<td>4.6%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a></td>
<td>0.9–1.0%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindi</a></td>
<td>0.7–3.9%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a></td>
<td>0.4–3.4%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Malagasy_language" title="Malagasy language">Malagasy</a></td>
<td>0.2–0.4%
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Omani_Arabic" title="Omani Arabic">Omani Arabic</a> is the source of most Arabic loanwords in Swahili.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In the text "Early Swahili History Reconsidered", however, Thomas Spear noted that Swahili retains a large amount of grammar, vocabulary, and sounds inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki language</a>. In fact, while taking account of daily vocabulary, using lists of one hundred words, 72–91% were inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki language</a> (which is reported as a parent language) whereas 4–17% were loan words from other African languages. Only 2–8% were from non-African languages, and Arabic loan words constituted a fraction of that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> According to other sources, around 40% of the Swahili vocabulary comes from Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> What also remained unconsidered was that a good number of the borrowed terms had native equivalents. The preferred use of Arabic loan words is prevalent along the coast, where natives, in a cultural show of proximity to, or descent from Arab culture, would rather use loan words, whereas the natives in the interior tend to use the native equivalents. It was originally written in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known documents written in Swahili are letters written in <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, in 1711 in the Arabic script that were sent to the Portuguese of Mozambique and their local allies. The original letters are preserved in the Historical Archives of Goa, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_period">Colonial period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colonial period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swahili-pn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="329" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Swahili-pn.jpg/170px-Swahili-pn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Swahili-pn.jpg/255px-Swahili-pn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Swahili-pn.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swahili-pn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Although originally written with the Arabic script, Swahili is now written in a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin alphabet</a> introduced by <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> and colonial administrators. The text shown here is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> version of the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Various colonial powers that ruled on the coast of East Africa played a role in the growth and spread of Swahili. With the arrival of the Arabs in East Africa, they used Swahili as a language of trade as well as for teaching Islam to the local <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu peoples</a>. This resulted in Swahili first being written in the Arabic alphabet. The later contact with the Portuguese resulted in the increase of vocabulary of the Swahili language. The language was formalised in an institutional level when the Germans took over after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_conference" title="Berlin conference">Berlin conference</a>. After seeing there was already a widespread language, the Germans formalised it as the official language to be used in schools. Thus schools in Swahili are called Shule (from German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Schule</i></span>) in government, trade and the court system. With the Germans controlling the major Swahili-speaking region in East Africa, they changed the alphabet system from Arabic to Latin. After the First World War, Britain took over German East Africa, where they found Swahili rooted in most areas, not just the coastal regions. The British decided to formalise it as the language to be used across the East African region (although in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">British East Africa</a> [Kenya and Uganda] most areas used English and various Nilotic and other Bantu languages while Swahili was mostly restricted to the coast). In June 1928, an inter-territorial conference attended by representatives of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(territory)" title="Tanganyika (territory)">Tanganyika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> took place in <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. The Zanzibar dialect was chosen as standard Swahili for those areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup> and the standard orthography for Swahili was adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Swahili has become a second language spoken by tens of millions in four <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> countries (<a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DRC" title="DRC">DRC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>), where it is an official or national language, while being the first language for many people in Tanzania especially in the coastal regions of Tanga, Pwani, Dar es Salaam, Mtwara and Lindi. In the inner regions of Tanzania, Swahili is spoken with an accent influenced by local languages and dialects, and as a first language for most people born in the cities, whilst being spoken as a second language in rural areas. Swahili and closely related languages are spoken by relatively small numbers of people in <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The language was still understood in the southern ports of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharusi_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kharusi-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Swahili speakers may number 150 to 200 million in total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> created an institution called the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=East_African_Kiswahili_Commission_(EACK)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="East African Kiswahili Commission (EACK) (page does not exist)">East African Kiswahili Commission (EACK)</a> which began operations in 2015. The institution currently serves as the leading body for promoting the language in the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African region</a>, as well as for coordinating its development and usage for regional integration and sustainable development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Press_Release_on_EAKC_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Release_on_EAKC-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Swahili is among the first languages in Africa for which <a href="/wiki/Language_technology" title="Language technology">language technology</a> applications have been developed. <a href="/wiki/Arvi_Hurskainen" title="Arvi Hurskainen">Arvi Hurskainen</a> is one of the early developers. The applications include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spelling_checker" title="Spelling checker">spelling checker</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Part-of-speech_tagging" title="Part-of-speech tagging">part-of-speech tagging</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_learning_software" title="Language learning software">language learning software</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> an analysed Swahili <a href="/wiki/Text_corpus" title="Text corpus">text corpus</a> of 25 million words,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_dictionary" title="Electronic dictionary">electronic dictionary</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> between Swahili and English. The development of language technology also strengthens the position of Swahili as a modern medium of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Furthermore, Swahili Wikipedia is among the few Wikipedias in African language featuring a fairly good amount of contributors and articles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tanzania">Tanzania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tanzania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The widespread use of Swahili as a national language in Tanzania came after Tanganyika gained independence in 1961 and the government decided that it would be used as a language to unify the new nation. This saw the use of Swahili in all levels of government, trade, art as well as schools in which primary school children are taught in Swahili, before switching to English (medium of instruction)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> in secondary schools (although Swahili is still taught as an independent subject).
After Tanganyika and Zanzibar unification in 1964, <i>Taasisi ya Uchunguzi wa Kiswahili</i> (TUKI, Institute of Swahili Research) was created from the Interterritorial Language Committee. In 1970 TUKI was merged with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Dar_es_Salaam" title="University of Dar es Salaam">University of Dar es Salaam</a>, while <i>Baraza la</i> <i>Kiswahili la Taifa</i> (BAKITA) was formed. BAKITA is an organisation dedicated to the development and advocacy of Swahili as a means of national integration in Tanzania. Key activities mandated for the organization include creating a healthy atmosphere for the development of Swahili, encouraging use of the language in government and business functions, coordinating activities of other organizations involved with Swahili, standardizing the language. BAKITA vision are: "1.To efficiently manage and coordinate the development and use of Kiswahili in Tanzania 2.To participate fully and effectively in promoting Swahili in East Africa, Africa and the entire world over".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Although other bodies and agencies can propose new vocabularies, BAKITA is the only organisation that can approve its usage in the Swahili language.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kenya">Kenya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Kenya">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Kenya, Kiswahili has been the national language since 1964 and is official since 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theeastafrican.co.ke_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-theeastafrican.co.ke-51">[51]</a></sup> <i>Chama cha Kiswahili cha Taifa</i> (CHAKITA) was established in 1998 to research and promote Kiswahili language in Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Kiswahili is a compulsory subject in all Kenyan primary and secondary schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uganda">Uganda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Uganda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Uganda adopted Kiswahili as the official language in 2022 and also made it compulsory across primary and secondary schools in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theeastafrican.co.ke_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-theeastafrican.co.ke-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Somalia">Somalia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Somalia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Swahili language is not widespread in Somalia and has no <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official status</a> nationally or regionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnolsom_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnolsom-56">[56]</a></sup> It is rarely taught in the education system, the main foreign languages are <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnolsom_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnolsom-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">Dialects</a> of Swahili are spoken by some <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">ethnic minorities</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_Islands" title="Bajuni Islands">Bajuni islands</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_dialect" title="Bajuni dialect">Kibajuni</a> on the southern tip of the country and in the town of <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Brava</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Bravanese_dialect" title="Bravanese dialect">Chimwiini</a>, both contain a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> loanwords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Standard Swahili is generally only spoken by Somali nationals who have resided in Kenya and subsequently returned to Somalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Lastly, a closely related language <a href="/wiki/Zigula_language" title="Zigula language">Mushunguli</a> (also known as Zigula, Zigua, or Chizigua) is spoken by some of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Bantus" title="Somali Bantus">Somali Bantu</a> ethnic minority mostly living in the <a href="/wiki/Jubba_Valley" title="Jubba Valley">Jubba Valley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnolsom_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnolsom-56">[56]</a></sup> It is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Coast_Bantu_languages" title="Northeast Coast Bantu languages">Northeast Coast Bantu language</a> as Swahili is and has some intelligibility with Swahili.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_and_political_identity">Religious and political identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Religious and political identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Swahili played a major role in spreading both <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">Islam</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>. From their arrival in East Africa, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> brought Islam and set up <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a>, where they used Swahili to teach Islam to the natives. As the Arab presence grew, more and more natives were converted to Islam and were taught using the Swahili language.
</p><p>From the arrival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rape_of_Africa" title="Rape of Africa">Europeans</a> in East Africa, Christianity was introduced to the region. While the Arabs were mostly based in the coastal areas, European missionaries went further inland spreading Christianity. As the first missionary posts in East Africa were in the coastal areas, missionaries picked up Swahili and used it to spread Christianity since it contained many similarities with other indigenous languages in the region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the struggle for Tanganyika independence, the <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_African_National_Union" title="Tanganyika African National Union">Tanganyika African National Union</a> used Swahili as language of mass organisation and political movement. This included publishing pamphlets and radio broadcasts to rally the people to fight for independence. After independence, Swahili was adopted as the national language of the nation. Till this day, Tanzanians carry a sense of pride when it comes to Swahili especially when it is used to unite over 120 tribes across Tanzania. Swahili was used to strengthen solidarity among the people and a sense of togetherness and for that Swahili remains a key identity of the Tanzanian people.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Standard Swahili has five vowel <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a>: <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>. According to <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Contini-Morava" title="Ellen Contini-Morava">Ellen Contini-Morava</a>, vowels are never <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">reduced</a>, regardless of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-62">[62]</a></sup> However, according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edgar_Polom%C3%A9" title="Edgar Polomé">Edgar Polomé</a>, these five phonemes can vary in pronunciation. Polomé claims that <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> are pronounced as such only in stressed syllables. In unstressed syllables, as well as before a prenasalized consonant, they are pronounced as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[e]</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ]</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[o]</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊ]</span>. <i>E</i> is also commonly pronounced as mid-position after <i>w</i>. Polomé claims that <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span> is pronounced as such only after <i>w</i> and is pronounced as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[a]</span> in other situations, especially after <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> (<i>y</i>). <i>A</i> can be pronounced as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ə]</span> in word-final position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-files.eric.ed.gov_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-files.eric.ed.gov-63">[63]</a></sup> Swahili vowels can be <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long</a>; these are written as two vowels (example: <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">kondoo</i></span>, meaning "sheep"). This is due to a historical process in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Voiced alveolar lateral approximant">L</a> became deleted between the second last and last vowel of a word ( e.g.<span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">kondoo</i></span> "sheep" was originally pronounced <i>kondolo</i>, which survives in certain dialects). However, these long vowels are not considered to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic" title="Phonemic">phonemic</a>. A similar process exists in <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language#Vowels" title="Zulu language">Zulu</a>.
</p>

<p>Some dialects of Swahili may also have the aspirated phonemes <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pʰ tʰ tʃʰ kʰ bʱ dʱ dʒʱ ɡʱ/</span> though they are unmarked in Swahili's orthography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodhi_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodhi-65">[65]</a></sup> Multiple studies favour classifying prenasalization as consonant clusters, not as separate phonemes. Historically, nasalization has been lost before voiceless consonants, and subsequently the voiced consonants have devoiced<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, though they are still written <i>mb, nd</i> etc. The /r/ phoneme is realised as either a short trill <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_trill" title="Voiced alveolar trill">r</a>]</span> or more commonly as a single tap <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_taps_and_flaps" title="Voiced dental and alveolar taps and flaps">ɾ</a>]</span> by most speakers. <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[x]</span> exists in free variation with h, and is only distinguished by some speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-files.eric.ed.gov_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-files.eric.ed.gov-63">[63]</a></sup> In some Arabic loans (nouns, verbs, adjectives), emphasis or intensity is expressed by reproducing the original emphatic consonants <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ, sˤ, tˤ, zˤ/</span> and the uvular <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/q/</span>, or lengthening a vowel, where aspiration would be used in inherited Bantu words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodhi_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lodhi-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="593" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swahili in Arabic script on the clothes of a girl in <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> (ca. early 1900s)</div></div></div>

<p>Swahili is now written in the Latin alphabet. There are a few <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">digraphs</a> for native sounds, <i>ch</i>, <i>sh</i>, <i>ng</i> and <i>ny</i>; <i>q</i> and <i>x</i> are not used,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <i>c</i> is not used apart from the digraph <i>ch</i>, unassimilated English loans and, occasionally, as a substitute for <i>k</i> in advertisements. There are also several digraphs for Arabic sounds, which many speakers outside of ethnic Swahili areas have trouble differentiating.
</p><p>The language used to be written in the <a href="/wiki/Ajami_script" title="Ajami script">Ajami script</a>, which is an Arabic script. Unlike other adaptations of the Arabic script for other languages, relatively little accommodation was made for Swahili. There were also differences in orthographic conventions between cities and authors and over the centuries, some quite precise but others different enough to cause difficulties with intelligibility.
</p><p><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> were often conflated, but in some spellings, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> was distinguished from <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> by rotating the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kasrah" title="Kasrah">kasra</a></i> 90° and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> was distinguished from <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> by writing the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Damma" title="Damma">damma</a></i> backwards.
</p><p>Several Swahili consonants do not have equivalents in Arabic, and for them, often no special letters were created unlike <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script#Additional_letters_used_in_other_languages" title="Arabic script">other languages</a>. Instead, the closest Arabic sound is substituted. Not only did that mean that one letter often stands for more than one sound, but also writers made different choices of which consonant to substitute. Below are some of the equivalents between Arabic Swahili and Roman Swahili:
</p>

<td><big><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ـظ</span>‎</big></big>

</td>
<td><big><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">يـ</span>‎</big></big>
</td>
<td><big><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D9%8A" title="ي">ي</a></span>‎</big></big>
</td>
<td>y ny
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>That was the general situation, but conventions from Urdu were adopted by some authors so as to distinguish aspiration and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span> from <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span>: <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">پھا</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pʰaa/</span> 'gazelle', <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">پا</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/paa/</span> 'roof'. Although it is not found in Standard Swahili today, there is a distinction between dental and <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar consonants</a> in some dialects, which is reflected in some orthographies, for example in <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">كُٹَ</span></span> <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">-kuta</i></span></i> 'to meet' vs. <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">كُتَ</span></span> <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">-kut̠a</i></span></i> 'to be satisfied'. A <i>k</i> with the dots of <i>y</i>, <span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ـػ</span>‎</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ـػـ</span>‎</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ػـ</span>‎</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; width: 1em;"><big><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ػ</span>‎</big></span>, was used for <i>ch</i> in some conventions; <i>ky</i> being historically and even contemporaneously a more accurate transcription than Roman <i>ch</i>. In Mombasa, it was common to use the Arabic emphatics for Cw, for example in <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">صِصِ</span></span> <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">swiswi</i></span></i> (standard <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">sisi</i></span></i>) 'we' and <span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sw-Arab">كِطَ</span></span> <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">kit̠wa</i></span></i> (standard <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">kichwa</i></span></i>) 'head'.
</p><p>Particles such as <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">ya, na, si, kwa, ni</i></span></i> are joined to the following noun, and possessives such as <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">yangu</i></span></i> and <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">yako</i></span></i> are joined to the preceding noun, but verbs are written as two words, with the subject and <a href="/wiki/Tense%E2%80%93aspect%E2%80%93mood" title="Tense–aspect–mood">tense–aspect–mood</a> morphemes separated from the object and root, as in <i><span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">aliyeniambia</i></span></i> "he who told me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_grammar" title="Swahili grammar">Swahili grammar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_classes">Noun classes <span class="anchor" id="Noun_classes"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Noun classes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Swahili nouns are separable into classes, which are roughly analogous to <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">genders</a> in other languages. In Swahili, <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefixes</a> mark groups of similar objects: ⟨m-⟩ marks single human beings (<span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">mtoto</i></span> 'child'), ⟨wa-⟩ marks multiple humans (<span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">watoto</i></span> 'children'), ⟨u-⟩ marks abstract nouns (<span title="Swahili (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="sw">utoto</i></span> 'childhood'), and so on. And just as adjectives and pronouns must agree with the gender of nouns in some languages with grammatical gender, so in Swahili adjectives, pronouns and even verbs must agree with nouns. This is a characteristic feature of all the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantic_motivation">Semantic motivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Semantic motivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>ki-/vi-</i> class historically consisted of two separate genders, artefacts (Bantu class 7/8, utensils and hand tools mostly) and diminutives (Bantu class 12/13), which were conflated at a stage ancestral to Swahili. Examples of the former are <i>kisu</i> "knife", <i>kiti</i> "chair" (from <i>mti</i> "tree, wood"), <i>chombo</i> "vessel" (a contraction of <i>ki-ombo</i>). Examples of the latter are <i>kitoto</i> "infant", from <i>mtoto</i> "child"; <i>kitawi</i> "frond", from <i>tawi</i> "branch"; and <i>chumba</i> (<i>ki-umba</i>) "room", from <i>nyumba</i> "house". It is the diminutive sense that has been furthest extended. An extension common to diminutives in many languages is <i>approximation</i> and <i>resemblance</i> (having a 'little bit' of some characteristic, like <i>-y</i> or <i>-ish</i> in English). For example, there is <i>kijani</i> "green", from <i>jani</i> "leaf" (compare English 'leafy'), <i>kichaka</i> "bush" from <i>chaka</i> "clump", and <i>kivuli</i> "shadow" from <i>uvuli</i> "shade". A 'little bit' of a verb would be an instance of an action, and such <i>instantiations</i> (usually not very active ones) are found: <i>kifo</i> "death", from the verb <i>-fa</i> "to die"; <i>kiota</i> "nest" from <i>-ota</i> "to brood"; <i>chakula</i> "food" from <i>kula</i> "to eat"; <i>kivuko</i> "a ford, a pass" from <i>-vuka</i> "to cross"; and <i>kilimia</i> "the <a href="/wiki/Pleiades" title="Pleiades">Pleiades</a>", from <i>-limia</i> "to farm with", from its role in guiding planting. A resemblance, or being a bit like something, implies marginal status in a category, so things that are marginal examples of their class may take the <i>ki-/vi-</i> prefixes. One example is <i>chura</i> (<i>ki-ura</i>) "frog", which is only half terrestrial and therefore is marginal as an animal. This extension may account for disabilities as well: <i>kilema</i> "a cripple", <i>kipofu</i> "a blind person", <i>kiziwi</i> "a deaf person". Finally, diminutives often denote contempt, and contempt is sometimes expressed against things that are dangerous. This might be the historical explanation for <i>kifaru</i> "<a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>", <i>kingugwa</i> "<a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyena</a>", and <i>kiboko</i> "<a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamus</a>" (perhaps originally meaning "stubby legs").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another class with broad semantic extension is the <i>m-/mi-</i> class (Bantu classes 3/4). This is often called the 'tree' class, because <i>mti, miti</i> "tree(s)" is the prototypical example. However, it seems to cover vital entities neither human nor typical animals: trees and other plants, such as <i>mwitu</i> 'forest' and <i>mtama</i> 'millet' (and from there, things made from plants, like <i>mkeka</i> 'mat'); supernatural and natural forces, such as <i>mwezi</i> 'moon', <i>mlima</i> 'mountain', <i>mto</i> 'river'; active things, such as <i>moto</i> 'fire', including active body parts (<i>moyo</i> 'heart', <i>mkono</i> 'hand, arm'); and human groups, which are vital but not themselves human, such as <i>mji</i> 'village', and, by analogy, <i>mzinga</i> 'beehive/cannon'. From the central idea of <i>tree</i>, which is thin, tall, and spreading, comes an extension to other long or extended things or parts of things, such as <i>mwavuli</i> 'umbrella', <i>moshi</i> 'smoke', <i>msumari</i> 'nail'; and from activity there even come active instantiations of verbs, such as <i>mfuo</i> "metal forging", from <i>-fua</i> "to forge", or <i>mlio</i> "a sound", from <i>-lia</i> "to make a sound". Words may be connected to their class by more than one metaphor. For example, <i>mkono</i> is an active body part, and <i>mto</i> is an active natural force, but they are also both long and thin. Things with a trajectory, such as <i>mpaka</i> 'border' and <i>mwendo</i> 'journey', are classified with long thin things, as in many other languages with noun classes. This may be further extended to anything dealing with time, such as <i>mwaka</i> 'year' and perhaps <i>mshahara</i> 'wages'. Animals exceptional in some way and so not easily fitting in the other classes may be placed in this class.
</p><p>The other classes have foundations that may at first seem similarly counterintuitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In short,
</p>
<ul><li>Classes 1–2 include most words for people: kin terms, professions, ethnicities, etc., including translations of most English words ending in <i>-er.</i> They include a couple of generic words for animals: <i>mnyama</i> 'beast', <i>mdudu</i> 'bug'.</li>
<li>Classes 5–6 have a broad semantic range of groups, expanses, and augmentatives. Although interrelated, it is easier to illustrate if broken down:
<ul><li>Augmentatives, such as <i>joka</i> 'serpent' from <i>nyoka</i> 'snake', lead to titles and other terms of respect (the opposite of diminutives, which lead to terms of contempt): <i>Bwana</i> 'Sir', <i>shangazi</i> 'aunt', <i>fundi</i> 'craftsman', <i>kadhi</i> 'judge'</li>
<li>Expanses: <i>ziwa</i> 'lake', <i>bonde</i> 'valley', <i>taifa</i> 'country', <i>anga</i> 'sky'
<ul><li>from this, mass nouns: <i>maji</i> 'water', <i>vumbi</i> 'dust' (and other liquids and fine particulates that may cover broad expanses), <i>kaa</i> 'charcoal', <i>mali</i> 'wealth', <i>maridhawa</i> 'abundance'</li></ul></li>
<li>Collectives: <i>kundi</i> 'group', <i>kabila</i> 'language/ethnic group', <i>jeshi</i> 'army', <i>daraja</i> ' stairs', <i>manyoya</i> 'fur, feathers', <i>mapesa</i> 'small change', <i>manyasi</i> 'weeds', <i>jongoo</i> 'millipede' (large set of legs), <i>marimba</i> 'xylophone' (large set of keys)
<ul><li>from this, individual things found in groups: <i>jiwe</i> 'stone', <i>tawi</i> 'branch', <i>ua</i> 'flower', <i>tunda</i> 'fruit' (also the names of most fruits), <i>yai</i> 'egg', <i>mapacha</i> 'twins', <i>jino</i> 'tooth', <i>tumbo</i> 'stomach' (cf. English "guts"), and paired body parts such as <i>jicho</i> 'eye', <i>bawa</i> 'wing', etc.</li>
<li>also collective or dialogic actions, which occur among groups of people: <i>neno</i> 'a word', from <i>kunena</i> 'to speak' (and by extension, mental verbal processes: <i>wazo</i> 'thought', <i>maana</i> 'meaning'); <i>pigo</i> 'a stroke, blow', from <i>kupiga</i> 'to hit'; <i>gomvi</i> 'a quarrel', <i>shauri</i> 'advice, plan', <i>kosa</i> 'mistake', <i>jambo</i> 'affair', <i>penzi</i> 'love', <i>jibu</i> 'answer', <i>agano</i> 'promise', <i>malipo</i> 'payment'</li>
<li>From pairing, reproduction is suggested as another extension (fruit, egg, testicle, flower, twins, etc.), but these generally duplicate one or more of the subcategories above</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Classes 9–10 are used for most typical animals: <i>ndege</i> 'bird', <i>samaki</i> 'fish', and the specific names of typical beasts, birds, and bugs. However, this is the 'other' class, for words not fitting well elsewhere, and about half of the class 9–10 nouns are foreign loanwords. Loans may be classified as 9–10 because they lack the prefixes inherent in other classes, and most native class 9–10 nouns have no prefix. Thus they do not form a coherent semantic class, though there are still semantic extensions from individual words.</li>
<li>Class 11 (which takes class 10 for the plural) are mostly nouns with an "extended outline shape", in either one dimension or two:
<ul><li>mass nouns that are generally localized rather than covering vast expanses: <i>uji</i> 'porridge', <i>wali</i> 'cooked rice'</li>
<li>broad: <i>ukuta</i> 'wall', <i>ukucha</i> 'fingernail', <i>upande</i> 'side' (≈ <i>ubavu</i> 'rib'), <i>wavu</i> 'net', <i>wayo</i> 'sole, footprint', <i>ua</i> 'fence, yard', <i>uteo</i> 'winnowing basket'</li>
<li>long: <i>utambi</i> 'wick', <i>utepe</i> 'stripe', <i>uta</i> 'bow', <i>ubavu</i> 'rib', <i>ufa</i> 'crack', <i>unywele</i> 'a hair'
<ul><li>from 'a hair', <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singulative" title="Singulative">singulatives</a> of nouns, which are often class 6 ('collectives') in the plural: <i>unyoya</i> 'a feather', <i>uvumbi</i> 'a mote of dust', <i>ushanga</i> 'a bead'.</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Class 14 are abstractions, such as <i>utoto</i> 'childhood' (from <i>mtoto</i> 'a child') and have no plural. They have the same prefixes and concord as class 11, except optionally for adjectival concord.</li>
<li>Class 15 are verbal infinitives.</li>
<li>Classes 16–18 are locatives. The Bantu nouns of these classes have been lost; the only permanent member is the Arabic loan <i>mahali</i> 'place(s)', but in Mombasa Swahili, the old prefixes survive: <i>pahali</i> 'place', <i>mwahali</i> 'places'. However, any noun with the locative suffix <i>-ni</i> takes class 16–18 agreement. The distinction between them is that class 16 agreement is used if the location is intended to be definite ("at"), class 17 if indefinite ("around") or involves motion ("to, toward"), and class 18 if it involves containment ("within"): <i>mahali pazuri</i> 'a good spot', <i>mahali kuzuri</i> 'a nice area', <i>mahali muzuri</i> (it's nice in there).</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Borrowing">Borrowing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Borrowing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Borrowings may or may not be given a prefix corresponding to the semantic class they fall in. For example, Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">دود</span></span> <i>dūd</i> ("bug, insect") was borrowed as <i>mdudu</i>, plural <i>wadudu</i>, with the class 1/2 prefixes <i>m-</i> and <i>wa-</i>, but Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">فلوس</span></span> <i>fulūs</i> ("fish scales", plural of <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">فلس</span></span> <i>fals</i>) and English <i>sloth</i> were borrowed as simply <i>fulusi</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Mahi-mahi" title="Mahi-mahi">mahi-mahi</a>" fish) and <i>slothi</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Sloth" title="Sloth">sloth</a>"), with no prefix associated with animals (whether those of class 9/10 or 1/2).
</p><p>In the process of naturalization<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> of borrowings within Swahili, loanwords are often reinterpreted, or reanalysed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCLE_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-LCLE-71">[71]</a></sup> as if they already contain a Swahili class prefix. In such cases the interpreted prefix is changed with the usual rules. Consider the following loanwords from Arabic:
</p>
<ol><li>The Swahili word for "book", <i>kitabu</i>, is borrowed from Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">كتاب</span></span> <i>kitāb(un)</i> "book" (plural <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">كتب</span></span> <i>kutub</i>; from the Arabic root <i>k.t.b.</i> "write"). However, the Swahili plural form of this word ("books") is <i><b>vi</b>tabu</i>, following Bantu grammar in which the <i>ki-</i> of <i><b>ki</b>tabu</i> is reanalysed (reinterpreted) as a nominal class prefix whose plural is <i>vi-</i> (class 7/8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCLE_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-LCLE-71">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li>Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">معلم</span></span> <i>muʿallim(un)</i> ("teacher", plural <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">معلمين</span></span> <i>muʿallimīna</i>) was interpreted as having the mw- prefix of class 1, and so became <i>mwalimu</i>, plural <i>walimu</i>.</li>
<li>Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">مدرسة</span></span> <i>madrasa</i> school, even though it is singular in Arabic (with plural <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">مدارس</span></span> <i>madāris</i>), was reinterpreted as a class 6 plural <i>madarasa</i>, receiving the singular form <i>darasa</i>.</li></ol>
<p>Similarly, English <i>wire</i> and Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">وقت</span></span> <i>waqt</i> ("time") were interpreted as having the class 11 prevocalic prefix <i>w-</i>, and became <i>waya</i> and <i>wakati</i> with plural <i>nyaya</i> and <i>nyakati</i> respectively.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agreement">Agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2016</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Swahili phrases agree with nouns in a system of <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">concord</a> but, if the noun refers to a human, they accord with noun classes 1–2 regardless of their noun class. Verbs agree with the noun class of their subjects and objects; adjectives, prepositions and demonstratives agree with the noun class of their nouns. In Standard Swahili <i>(Kiswahili sanifu)</i>, based on the dialect spoken in Zanzibar, the system is rather complex; however, it is drastically simplified in many local variants where Swahili is not a native language, such as in Nairobi. In non-native Swahili, concord reflects only animacy: human subjects and objects trigger <i>a-, wa-</i> and <i>m-, wa-</i> in verbal concord, while non-human subjects and objects of whatever class trigger <i>i-, zi-</i>. Infinitives vary between standard <i>ku-</i> and reduced <i>i-.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> ("Of" is animate <i>wa</i> and inanimate <i>ya, za.</i>)
</p><p>In Standard Swahili, human subjects and objects of whatever class trigger animacy concord in <i>a-, wa-</i> and <i>m-, wa-,</i> and non-human subjects and objects trigger a variety of gender-concord prefixes.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most Swahili adjectives begin with either a consonant or the vowels <i>i-</i> or <i>e-</i>, listed separately above. The few adjectives beginning with other vowels do not agree with all noun classes since some are restricted to humans. NC 1 <i>m(w)-</i> is <i>mw-</i> before <i>a</i> and <i>o,</i> and reduces to <i>m-</i> before <i>u</i>; <i>wa-</i> does not change; and <i>ki-, vi-, mi-</i> become <i>ch-, vy-, my-</i> before <i>o</i> but not before <i>u</i>: <i>mwanana, waanana</i> "gentle", <i>mwororo, waororo, myororo, chororo, vyororo</i> "mild, yielding", <i>mume, waume, kiume, viume</i> "male".</span>
